      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer7739625226490578662">Fletch<br /><br />He’s an investigative reporter whose methods are a little unorthodox. Currently he’s living on the beach with the strung-out trying to find to the source of the drugs they live for. <br /><br />Fletch<br /><br />He’s taking more than a little flack from his editor. She doesn’t appreciate his style. Or the expense account items he’s racking up. Or his definition of the word <em>deadline</em>. Or the</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7739625226490578662">Fletch<br /><br />He’s an investigative reporter whose methods are a little unorthodox. Currently he’s living on the beach with the strung-out trying to find to the source of the drugs they live for. <br /><br />Fletch<br /><br />He’s taking more than a little flack from his editor. She doesn’t appreciate his style. Or the expense account items he’s racking up. Or his definition of the word <em>deadline</em>. Or the divorce lawyers who keep showing up at the office.<br /><br />Fletch<br /><br />So when multimillionaire Alan Stanwyk offers Fletch the job of a lifetime, which could be worth a fortune, he’s intrigued and decides to do a little investigation. What he discovers is that the proposition is anything but what it seems.</span>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_50626056">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/50626056" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/405390-kemper" class="left imgcol" title="Kemper"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1451574879p2/405390.jpg" alt="Kemper" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/50626056?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 26, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/405390-kemper" class="user" title="Kemper">Kemper</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=crime-mystery" class="actionLinkLite">crime-mystery</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=humor" class="actionLinkLite">humor</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=2011-r" class="actionLinkLite">2011-r</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=rubbermaid-treasure" class="actionLinkLite">rubbermaid-treasure</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer50626056">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2541737538977737391"><strong>Treasure of the Rubbermaids 5: The Underhill Account</strong><br /><br /><em>The on-going discoveries of priceless books and comics found in a stack of Rubbermaid containers previously stored and forgotten at my parent’s house and untouched for almost 20 years. Thanks to my father dumping them back on me, I now spend my spare time unearthing lost treasures from their plastic depths.</em><br /><br />I don’t know what it is in the DNA of American males that makes so many of us quote movies incessantly. There are certain friends of mine th</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2541737538977737391"><strong>Treasure of the Rubbermaids 5: The Underhill Account</strong><br /><br /><em>The on-going discoveries of priceless books and comics found in a stack of Rubbermaid containers previously stored and forgotten at my parent’s house and untouched for almost 20 years. Thanks to my father dumping them back on me, I now spend my spare time unearthing lost treasures from their plastic depths.</em><br /><br />I don’t know what it is in the DNA of American males that makes so many of us quote movies incessantly. There are certain friends of mine that I can have entire conversations with that consist of nothing but repeating lines from films like <em>Animal House, Caddyshack, Ghostbusters</em> and a hundred others. One of the all-time richest veins of movie quotes is <em>Fletch</em>.<br /><br />After the movie came out in 1985, my friends and I watched it dozens of times on video and my fifteen year old self was delighted to learn that there was a whole series of books that the movie was based on. I found a movie tie-in copy that made me giggle with glee at the prospect of getting whole books of more Fletch stories. <br /><br />However, as is often the case, the book and the movie have some serious differences that threw me for a loop. As readers, we’ve all been disgusted when a movie version of a book we love made stupid changes that ruined what we liked about it. Very rarely, a movie may make some changes that improve the story. But Fletch is an odd case because in some ways the movie is very much like the book, but at the same time it isn’t. Yet both ended up being remarkably good. Weird, huh?<br /><br />The main plot of both remains the same. Irwin Fletcher is a smart ass investigative reporter who has gone undercover as a junkie to find the source of a drug epidemic at the beach. In his role as an addict, Fletch is approached by a wealthy man named Alan Stanwyk who makes him an incredible offer. Stanwyk claims to be dying of cancer and has set up an elaborate plot to have someone murder him so that his insurance won’t be nullified by suicide. The offer to kill him comes with a large sum of money and an elaborate escape plan.<br /><br />Fletch plays along and agrees to kill Stanwyk. Then he embarks on a clandestine investigation to find out if the man really is dying of cancer while still trying to figure out the source of the drugs on the beach as his editor demands that he print what he already knows.<br /><br />Sounds like the movie, right? Yes and no. Because while the main plot is the same, it’s Chevy Chase’s portrayal of Fletch that changes the tone. While this remains one of his best movies, Chase was essentially doing what he always did; acting like Chevy Chase. He’s a quick witted smart ass who occasionally behaves like a complete doofus. The running gag of him pretending to be various people let him act like a dumbass while still being the smug guy who is playing everyone.<br /><br />The Fletch of the book is also a quick witted smart ass, but he isn’t a goofball. While he frequently lies about his identity to get information, there’s no silly disguises or acting the fool. This Fletch is also a Vietnam vet with more problems than the Chevy Chase version. His fights with his incompetent editor are more serious and not playful, and the alimony of two failed marriages is a real problem and not just a joke.<br /><br />And to be blunt, the book version of Fletch is kind of a prick. (From what I’ve read about Chevy Chase, he is also kind of prick, but he didn’t showcase that in this movie.) Fletch is the kind of guy who threw his ex-wife’s cat out a seventh story window when he got sick of the cat smell and doesn’t apologize for it. He’s funny but mean to most of the people he deals with. Granted, many of them are assholes, but book Fletch is a bit nastier than Chevy Fletch.<br /><br />And this Fletch does things like shack up with a fifteen year runaway prostitute. The book never makes it clear that he was having sex with her, but they are sharing a sleeping bag so while there is a feeling that maybe he was trying to protect her and his cover, it’s still pretty damn creepy. <br /><br />The book also ends differently with a much darker twist than the movie version did. All in all, it seems like Hollywood took a good mystery with some funny lines and a dark undercurrent to it, including a flawed main character, then they sanded off the rough edges and brought in Chevy Chase, who did what worked for him rather than making an effort to portray it as written in the book.<br /><br />What we got was a movie that concentrated on the humor and became a comedy classic while the book remains a good crime story that will probably leave fans of the movie who read it slightly unsettled at some of it’s darker plot twists.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2541737538977737391">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="xhr_comment_photo">
    	<a href="/user/show/3483907-james-thane" alt="James Thane"><img alt="3483907" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442513566p1/3483907.jpg" /></a>
  </div>
  <div class="xhr_comment_body">
    <a href="/user/show/3483907-james-thane" style="font-weight:bold">James Thane</a>

      
<span id="freeTextContainer6231917464045393437">Yeah, I remember discovering all of these books at about the same time. The first Spenser (1973) and the first Fletch (1974) appeared almost simultane</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6231917464045393437">Yeah, I remember discovering all of these books at about the same time. The first Spenser (1973) and the first Fletch (1974) appeared almost simultaneously. Even though the first McGee appeared in 1964, I didn't start reading them until the early '70s. I confess that, at that time, MacDonald's treatment of women flew right over my head too, although even then I found it hard to believe that McGee would be consistently <em>that</em> attractive to women.<br /><br />In an atttempt at fairness, I would argue that the McGee books are a product of their times in terms of their treatment of women and I'm sure you would find the same attitude--or worse--displayed in a lot of the "men's fiction", pulp and otherwise of the period. But it is hard to take now, whether it's MacDonald writing it or anyone else.<br /><br />Like Kristine, I also devoured the Perry Mason books, starting from when I was just a kid. My father loved them and I was constantly picking them up when he was finished. These were the first "crime novels" I ever read and I actually managed to collect all 85 of them. I still have a strong sentimental attachment to them, but reading them now often leaves me shaking my head becuse the plots are often so convoluted and nonsensical.</span>

      <a href="/user/show/6637209-cathy-dupont" class="user" title="Cathy DuPont">Cathy DuPont</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/994727.Fletch" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780380006458" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer513395176">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5510298336336020482">Dear Friends:<br /><br />I read 141 books last year, however, regretfully, I did not meet my challenge. Thus far this year, I've read nine books including this book, Fletch, published in 1974. <br /><br />Out of all those books, this might be the most entertaining of them all. <br /><br />It had the elements I enjoy so much in reading books: a mystery; great character development; a very likable protag who has a multitude of personal issues particularly two ex-wives who he owes alimony; a respected investigative journalist who is</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5510298336336020482">Dear Friends:<br /><br />I read 141 books last year, however, regretfully, I did not meet my challenge. Thus far this year, I've read nine books including this book, Fletch, published in 1974. <br /><br />Out of all those books, this might be the most entertaining of them all. <br /><br />It had the elements I enjoy so much in reading books: a mystery; great character development; a very likable protag who has a multitude of personal issues particularly two ex-wives who he owes alimony; a respected investigative journalist who is 'bulldog' tough and doesn't let go of a great story; lousey publishers who call the shots however know little to nothing about journalism; and last but not least, humor; light humor but nevertheless, it was just plain funny in parts. <br /><br />Fletch has been on my shelf for years and I never thought about picking it up and reading it but I just got tired of looking at the much worn paperback cover, so wanted to read it, get it out of my house to replace with a better read. (Silly me, little did I know.) When I looked at it more closely, I noticed it was an Edgar Award Winner.<br /><br />The Edgars are given yearly to mysteries and like those, including my friend <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/user/show/1526851-will">Will Byrnes</a> who watches all the Oscar nominees, I try to read as many Edgar nominees and winners as possible. <br /><br />Terrible that I have passed this book up for so many years but very happy today that I read it because it was a wild ride with Fletch, one character I will not forget for a long time.<br /><br />As an aside, a movie was made from the book starring Chevy Chase as Fletch. Great casting because I can see Chase as Fletch which should give my friends an idea of the humor I mentioned above. <br /><br />Good reading, everyone, and pick this one up next time you see it; it was worthy of the Edgar Award, the Oscar in mystery books. <br /><br />Cathy</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5510298336336020482">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_55330463">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/55330463" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4695-daniel" class="left imgcol" title="Daniel"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1264806364p2/4695.jpg" alt="Daniel" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/55330463?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 16, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4695-daniel" class="user" title="Daniel">Daniel</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4695-daniel?shelf=montrose-book-guy" class="actionLinkLite">montrose-book-guy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4695-daniel?shelf=2009" class="actionLinkLite">2009</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer55330463">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8937131338769009512">"Fletch" is a quick read and completely satisfying as a mystery. Gregory Mcdonald skillfully juggles the novel's multiple plots and threads the two main ones together at the end without straining credulity. Even 35 years after its publication, "Fletch" still feels original: its main character is a newspaper reporter rather than the typical cop or private investigator, and the book never loses its sense of fun, a quality so often lacking in detective novels. Mcdonald's writing style is suitably t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8937131338769009512">"Fletch" is a quick read and completely satisfying as a mystery. Gregory Mcdonald skillfully juggles the novel's multiple plots and threads the two main ones together at the end without straining credulity. Even 35 years after its publication, "Fletch" still feels original: its main character is a newspaper reporter rather than the typical cop or private investigator, and the book never loses its sense of fun, a quality so often lacking in detective novels. Mcdonald's writing style is suitably terse for a story about a reporter, and Irwin Fletcher's own writing within the book is exactly right for newspapers of the time. Few if any of the book's words are wasted.<br /><br />It's a bit disappointing to know that "Fletch" is the first in a series. The book is so complete and ends so perfectly that I almost don't want to know how Mcdonald manages to bring Irwin Fletcher back in the first sequel. I've read that the sequels provide diminishing returns, and don't find that surprising given what a fantastic job Mcdonald did with the first book.<br /><br />So why four stars instead of five? Mostly because "Fletch" doesn't possess those extra qualities that make it rise beyond being a genre novel, such as the wonderful metaphors and moral weight that make Chandler's "The Big Sleep" and "Farewell, My Lovely" five-star books for me. Nevertheless, "Fletch" is about as good as a detective book can be without also being something more, and that's still pretty damned good.<br /><br />By the way, I can't really compare the book to the 1985 Chevy Chase movie. I saw it when it first came out and maybe again a couple years after that, but don't remember it that well. I do recall it being goofier and more of a straightforward comedy than the book is.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8937131338769009512">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/81752931" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1177534-michael" class="left imgcol" title="Michael"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1428281677p2/1177534.jpg" alt="Michael" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/81752931?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 01, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1177534-michael" class="user" title="Michael">Michael</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1177534-michael?shelf=mystery" class="actionLinkLite">mystery</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1177534-michael?shelf=read-in-2009" class="actionLinkLite">read-in-2009</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer81752931">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3287742204704630470">I recently caught the classic Chevy Chase movie version of "Fletch" on cable, which served to remind me that I'd never actually read the original source material. <br /><br />So, I rushed off to my local library and checked out "Fletch."<br /><br />The novel itself is pretty much the same basic plot as the film, though there are some differences. <br /><br />The big difference is how dialogue driven the novel is. Gregory Macdonald tells the story with minimal descriptive paragraphs and instead allows the reader to discover things</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3287742204704630470">I recently caught the classic Chevy Chase movie version of "Fletch" on cable, which served to remind me that I'd never actually read the original source material. <br /><br />So, I rushed off to my local library and checked out "Fletch."<br /><br />The novel itself is pretty much the same basic plot as the film, though there are some differences. <br /><br />The big difference is how dialogue driven the novel is. Gregory Macdonald tells the story with minimal descriptive paragraphs and instead allows the reader to discover things through the use of various conversations taking place between Irwin Fletcher and whomever he's talking to. For the most part it works fairly well. Macdonald has a good ear for dialogue and most of the conversations have echoes of the wit of Fletch. (It's hard to read and not hear Chevy Chase delivering Fletch's dialogue). <br /><br />The story unfolds well enough and the central mystery is an intriguing one. I can see why the series has been so popular and well remembered by fans. I can also see why there's a passion for more big-screen exploits by Fletch himself. <br /><br />I may have to look for more of this series in 2010. </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1281468757">

  <a href="/user/show/12116649-jonathan" class="left imgcol" title="Jonathan"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1389547509p2/12116649.jpg" alt="Jonathan" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1281468757?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 25, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/12116649-jonathan" class="user" title="Jonathan">Jonathan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/12116649-jonathan?shelf=mystery" class="actionLinkLite">mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1281468757">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3836487661414984719"><strong>Recommended for: Pulp Fiction lovers (not the movie); fans of clever and irreverent main characters; people not offended by old-fashioned patriarchal attitudes.</strong><br /><br />All I knew with regards to this book before reading was the movie that was based on it. While reading, my respect for Chevy Chase's charm and comedic talents was refreshed. The book is <em>very</em> different from the movie.<br /><br />In the film, Fletch is essentially the same character-- he's womanizing, reckless, and clever. But in the book the classic ma</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3836487661414984719"><strong>Recommended for: Pulp Fiction lovers (not the movie); fans of clever and irreverent main characters; people not offended by old-fashioned patriarchal attitudes.</strong><br /><br />All I knew with regards to this book before reading was the movie that was based on it. While reading, my respect for Chevy Chase's charm and comedic talents was refreshed. The book is <em>very</em> different from the movie.<br /><br />In the film, Fletch is essentially the same character-- he's womanizing, reckless, and clever. But in the book the classic male aloofness has the darker edge that was common to pulp detectives. He's got the murky past that, though never fully explained, is intended to justify his current approach to life. And that approach includes a distaste for almost everyone around him, the desire not to get close to anyone, to make light of serious situations. It is engaging, though not, as one reviewer on Goodreads put it, likeable. He would be the classic Han Solo scoundrel type if he, like Han Solo, also showed weakness and fell for a Princess Leia. But there's none of that. One of his casual flings dies in his sleeping bag in his apartment and he's barely more than inconvenienced by it. Long story short, the movie Fletch was more likeable.<br /><br />Still...<br /><br />This book is a well-executed mystery, with clues to get you thinking but not give away the ultimate end-game. It's a fast read, an entertaining read, and while it won't be the most memorable piece of fiction you ever read, the ratio of quality to time investment is completely acceptable.<br /><br />3.5 stars. A little more than liked it.<br />4 stars for the movie. ;)</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3836487661414984719">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_768717062">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/768717062" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/12761919-col" class="left imgcol" title="Col"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1349328590p2/12761919.jpg" alt="Col" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/768717062?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 15, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/12761919-col" class="user" title="Col">Col</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/12761919-col?shelf=2013" class="actionLinkLite">2013</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/12761919-col?shelf=m" class="actionLinkLite">m</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer768717062">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17264744939826360325">Synopsis/blurb......<br /><br />Fletch<br />He’s an investigative reporter whose methods are a little unorthodox. Currently he’s living on the beach with the strung-out trying to find to the source of the drugs they live for. <br /><br />Fletch<br />He’s taking more than a little flack from his editor. She doesn’t appreciate his style. Or the expense account items he’s racking up. Or his definition of the word deadline. Or the divorce lawyers who keep showing up at the office.<br /><br />Fletch<br />So when multimillionaire Alan Stanwyk offers Flet</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17264744939826360325">Synopsis/blurb......<br /><br />Fletch<br />He’s an investigative reporter whose methods are a little unorthodox. Currently he’s living on the beach with the strung-out trying to find to the source of the drugs they live for. <br /><br />Fletch<br />He’s taking more than a little flack from his editor. She doesn’t appreciate his style. Or the expense account items he’s racking up. Or his definition of the word deadline. Or the divorce lawyers who keep showing up at the office.<br /><br />Fletch<br />So when multimillionaire Alan Stanwyk offers Fletch the job of a lifetime, which could be worth a fortune, he’s intrigued and decides to do a little investigation. What he discovers is that the proposition is anything but what it seems.<br />-------------------------<br /><br />My take.........<br /><br />This book was originally published back in the mid-70’s and won McDonald an Edgar Award for best first novel in 1975. (The second in the series, Confess, Fletch won him a second Edgar.)<br /><br />An interesting mystery, written at a time when 200 pages was probably the norm for the genre. (Don’t you long for those days?) Enough time to develop the main character and get a decent feel for the rest of the cast and create an intriguing plot.<br /><br />Irwin Fletcher, intrepid investigative journalist is masquerading as a beach bum, trying to get a story of the drug’s trade being plied on the small town (name escapes me!) beach. He’s approached by a man and offered $20k to kill him.<br /><br />Fletcher decides to accept the offer and has a week to uncover the raison d’etre for the mystery man’s deathwish. In the meantime, his bosses are impatient at the lack of progress on his drug’s investigation and he walks a fine line regarding his job longetivity. In the background he’s also juggling law suits and court appearances regarding the non-payment of alimony to his two ex-wives, both of whom are still in love with him.<br /><br />Great fun had here, as Fletcher juggles all the balls to get the scoop on the drugs and after an unorthodox inquiry finally discovers millionaire Stanwyck’s motives for hiring out his own murder.<br /><br />Fletcher is the obvious star, generally likeable, though you can call me a prude if you like, I wasn’t a particular fan of his actions when dealing with his former wives......a little bit too dismissive and blasé for my liking, but probably all the more realistic for having these warts.<br /><br />I will definitely be reading more from McDonald, though when I’m not too sure. My copy of this is an omnibus edition and contains the first 3 Fletcher books. McDonald wrote 9 in total, as well as a couple of son of Fletch books and a 4 book Flynn series, which apparently is a spin-off featuring a cop we encounter further down the road with our man Irwin Fletcher.<br /><br />Full list of the books is as follows, thanks to Fantastic Fiction:<br /><br />Fletch<br />1. Fletch (1974)<br />2. Confess, Fletch (1976)<br />3. Fletch's Fortune (1979)<br />4. Fletch and the Widow Bradley (1981)<br />5. Fletch's Moxie (1982)<br />6. Fletch and the Man Who (1983)<br />7. Carioca Fletch (1984)<br />8. Fletch Won (1985)<br />9. Fletch Too (1986)<br />Flynn<br />1. Flynn (1978)<br />2. The Buck Passes Flynn (1981)<br />3. Flynn's in (1984)<br />4. Flynn's World (1999)<br />Son of Fletch<br />1. Son of Fletch (1993)<br />2. Fletch Reflected (1994<br /><br />I can’t recall ever seeing either of the films based on Gregory McDonald’s Fletch character, but I can say I have now read the first in the series. Is it heresy to say, I’m not a great fan of Chevy Chase? I did enjoy a few of the Vacation movies, but that’s pretty much it as far as he is concerned. I would now be interested in watching the film of this though.<br /><br />I have all of the original series of books on my shelves, all acquired cheaply after a friend sent me the omnibus edition, telling me to check Fletch out. Great advice! (Sometimes it pays to take a chance when book buying, re the other 6 I have!)<br /><br />4 from 5 and a great way to round off a brilliant year’s reading!<br /><br />As mentioned above, my copy was gifted to me by a friend. Cheers to Cathy in Florida!</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1329685156">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1329685156" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/9805960-dipanjan" class="left imgcol" title="Dipanjan"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1394426361p2/9805960.jpg" alt="Dipanjan" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1329685156?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 29, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/9805960-dipanjan" class="user" title="Dipanjan">Dipanjan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1329685156">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14498354890789958206">"Fletch" introduces Fletch, an investigative reporter who happens to be the types I can relate to. Intelligent, cocky, obnoxious, witty and always a step ahead. The setup is pretty odd when an industrialist hires Fletch to murder him while Fletch was undercover on the beach to bust a drug racket. His attitude oozes out from the very first page. Mr. McDonald surely knows how to punch in a biting and sarcastic humour with a very real and genuine mystery. The book is all about the "dialogue". There</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14498354890789958206">"Fletch" introduces Fletch, an investigative reporter who happens to be the types I can relate to. Intelligent, cocky, obnoxious, witty and always a step ahead. The setup is pretty odd when an industrialist hires Fletch to murder him while Fletch was undercover on the beach to bust a drug racket. His attitude oozes out from the very first page. Mr. McDonald surely knows how to punch in a biting and sarcastic humour with a very real and genuine mystery. The book is all about the "dialogue". There is plenty brains, plenty cockiness and the women are ravishing. The other thing you will love is Fletch's complete disdain for incompetence in his professional supervisors. His disdain is not towards authority but towards the incompetence the authorities. His interactions with Clara and Frank are hilarious. Though there are two separate plot-lines, Mr. McDonald delivers a gripping novel where the plots don't converge much. You can go ahead and expect some SOLID DETECTIVE WORK which is complete value for your time and money. Fletch borders somewhere between naughty and wicked. He is a master of entrapment.<br /><br />You can't miss this series. So looking forward to the next Fletch Run.<br /><br />P.S - Please avoid the movie. It's complete garbage compared to the wonder of the reading experience.<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14498354890789958206">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1292220727">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1292220727" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5548263-bookworm-smith" class="left imgcol" title="Bookworm Smith"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1311819501p2/5548263.jpg" alt="Bookworm Smith" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1292220727?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 28, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5548263-bookworm-smith" class="user" title="Bookworm Smith">Bookworm Smith</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1292220727">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7478039447228793443">Alrighty, this book - ten point three times more than expected! One of those rare plot driven AND character driven stories...not an easy thing to pull off.<br /><br />It's funny, I picked up this book at the Bibles for Missions, for a whole fifty cents, expecting a comedy. I vaguely recalled the Fletch movie with Chevy Chase, picturing a farcical take on a private investigator...boy, was my memory off...furthermore, the cover of the book had some blurbs proclaiming it to be the funniest novel in recent hist</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7478039447228793443">Alrighty, this book - ten point three times more than expected! One of those rare plot driven AND character driven stories...not an easy thing to pull off.<br /><br />It's funny, I picked up this book at the Bibles for Missions, for a whole fifty cents, expecting a comedy. I vaguely recalled the Fletch movie with Chevy Chase, picturing a farcical take on a private investigator...boy, was my memory off...furthermore, the cover of the book had some blurbs proclaiming it to be the funniest novel in recent history! I have to say, after finishing the book my sides did not hurt. Repeat: Did Not Hurt...as in there was no laughter...no hahas!<br />What I did get out of the book was a brain twisting mystery solved at the last moment, a dive into the world of 70's journalism, and a hate on for the main character (who is a complete jerk, liar, and brilliant investigator)!<br />The plot is a brilliant weave of two stories that collide into fireworks at the end. A story of corrupt cops dealing drugs and an ultra rich family swindling everyone with a faked suicide. From the opening scene - promising young CEO asking a drug addicted beach bum to assist him in his own suicide - you'd never guess it would end (not go give it away) the way it does.<br />The story progresses in one of those casual ways where the clues are slowly doled out to the reader, without their knowing, until right at the end where everything fuses together. It's a story you wish you could read twice, or have taken notes along the way, or just wish you had a super bionic memory that could hold every snippet of info and figure out the puzzle before it becomes plainly obvious. I don't have a bionic memory, so I should have taken some notes.<br />Pushing this story along, and prying out the clues along the way is Fletch. Fletch is the super hero of journalists. Bringing in big stories that sell papers. However, he is pushy, arrogant, jerky, self centred, mean, rude, untrustworthy...and worst of all - abuses animals! But, he is so good at talking to people. He can trick, manipulate, whatever, in order to get the answers he is looking for. What a journalist!<br />One good read.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7478039447228793443">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3978317702084640763">I just reread Fletch after probably 20 years of not reading it. I have read other titles by Gregory McDonald in that period, but Fletch had gathered dust on my shelves for 20 years. Big mistake on my part. <br /><br />Coming back after 20 years, this book still stands as one of the best mysteries ever written, period. End of story. <br /><br />McDonald's snappy dialogue is the best in the business. He reveals character and so much more just through dialogue. Writer, director, and producer Kevin Smith say he learned to</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3978317702084640763">I just reread Fletch after probably 20 years of not reading it. I have read other titles by Gregory McDonald in that period, but Fletch had gathered dust on my shelves for 20 years. Big mistake on my part. <br /><br />Coming back after 20 years, this book still stands as one of the best mysteries ever written, period. End of story. <br /><br />McDonald's snappy dialogue is the best in the business. He reveals character and so much more just through dialogue. Writer, director, and producer Kevin Smith say he learned to write dialogue by reading Gregory McDonald. <br /><br />Fletch is flawed, and sometimes just wrong-headed, but he is real. <br /><br />Now coming back and reading this after having had to deal with a couple of hack editors, I can appreciate more Fletch's frustrations with one of his editors. <br /><br />This is still great writing. </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer88551429">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15005897204751827270">Entertainment Weekly just wrote about Fletch (the movie), raving about how it is totally this cult classic and everyone loves it so much. I don't remember loving the movie--probably because A) I read the books first and B) I don't crush on Chevy Chase--though now I'm willing to watch it again just out of curiosity. But what I *really* wanted to do after reading the article was read the books again! <br /><br />OK, I finished it quick. This is even more fun to read now than it was when I discovered it 20-odd</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15005897204751827270">Entertainment Weekly just wrote about Fletch (the movie), raving about how it is totally this cult classic and everyone loves it so much. I don't remember loving the movie--probably because A) I read the books first and B) I don't crush on Chevy Chase--though now I'm willing to watch it again just out of curiosity. But what I *really* wanted to do after reading the article was read the books again! <br /><br />OK, I finished it quick. This is even more fun to read now than it was when I discovered it 20-odd years ago. No cell phones, no laptops, just a portable tape deck and a typewriter in a suitcase, and a dash to a landline in critical moments. And it's still a total crash-course in writing dialog: seems like 90% of the story is moved forward in conversation, not exposition. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11736244824517515080">You've got to like Fletch, the edgy, hard-boiled news reporter. This story uses lots of twists but our man Fletch is on top of them all. Clipped dialogue has flashes of humor. Great entertainment. The author Gregory Mcdonald who won back-to-back Edgars recently passed away. Bottom line: I'll read deeper into the series.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10495263685605148644">As fresh as it ever was, <em>Fletch</em> is on helluva fast and fun read. I first came across these books in my Air Force days in the mid-eighties. Having gotten an e-book of the first book I turned to this after finishing Walter Lord's account of Dunkirk for some lighter reading. What a joy this is. McDonald's prose just flows along a rapidly moving story. I remember the first paperback I bought of this had the title on the cover and the opening lines from the story there as well. Great hook. Lots of di</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10495263685605148644">As fresh as it ever was, <em>Fletch</em> is on helluva fast and fun read. I first came across these books in my Air Force days in the mid-eighties. Having gotten an e-book of the first book I turned to this after finishing Walter Lord's account of Dunkirk for some lighter reading. What a joy this is. McDonald's prose just flows along a rapidly moving story. I remember the first paperback I bought of this had the title on the cover and the opening lines from the story there as well. Great hook. Lots of dialog here and a fast pace and Fletch, journalist extraordinaire discovers the source of drugs on the beach while figuring out why a prominent and wealthy young man has hired Fletch to kill him. Just fantastic stuff. What fun it was to re-read this after so many years. Ahhhhhh!!!!</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13028279919121821623">As a writer, I was fascinated by how Gregory McDonald used dialogue to carry most of this story.<br /><br />But as a reader, I was sort of bored and detached throughout most of this. Ironically the biggest problem was the fact that it was mostly dialogue. It was all on the surface, no real depth to be found here, which is a shame because I wanted to know more about Fletch, but this is not that kind of book. This is a quick mystery story with an interesting lead, and not much else, which is fine if that soun</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13028279919121821623">As a writer, I was fascinated by how Gregory McDonald used dialogue to carry most of this story.<br /><br />But as a reader, I was sort of bored and detached throughout most of this. Ironically the biggest problem was the fact that it was mostly dialogue. It was all on the surface, no real depth to be found here, which is a shame because I wanted to know more about Fletch, but this is not that kind of book. This is a quick mystery story with an interesting lead, and not much else, which is fine if that sounds like your kind of thing. <br /><br />It's just not mine.<br /><br />I'm glad to have read this. Though I will not be continuing with the series.</span>

      <a href="/user/show/19118048-aileen" class="user" title="Aileen">Aileen</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1442599591">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16569474624792430650">I was looking for another multi-book series with great dialogue to sink my teeth into after finishing up the Spenser novels. I muddled along through the first Fletch and will probably tackle the second, but can't say they measure up to Robert B. Parker's standard.<br />As an earlier commenter noted, Fletch is definitely a product of the times (mid 1970s), so if that default patriarchal attitude is a problem, you might want to skip this one. The dialogue is strong and there are charming moments. But I</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16569474624792430650">I was looking for another multi-book series with great dialogue to sink my teeth into after finishing up the Spenser novels. I muddled along through the first Fletch and will probably tackle the second, but can't say they measure up to Robert B. Parker's standard.<br />As an earlier commenter noted, Fletch is definitely a product of the times (mid 1970s), so if that default patriarchal attitude is a problem, you might want to skip this one. The dialogue is strong and there are charming moments. But I wouldn't say they outweigh the casual chauvinism that makes the whole endeavour feel dated.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16569474624792430650">...more</a>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16938921215636537037">One of the interesting things about Gregory McDonald books is his writing style. 90% of the story is told through dialogue. But Fletch doesn't care. The only thing that matters is getting the story out to the newspaper reading public. <br /><br />A really great character and a really good read. </span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1213519719">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3556678992078564604">Of course, Hollywood doesn't get it right. Each time that I watched the movie, "Fletch"; there is a credit to the book from which the movie comes. Actually, I have had this book in my library for quite some time. Only about a month ago (January 2015), I pulled the book out and read it through. I am not sure why these things happen, but the names of the secondary characters are different. The "Chief of Police" in the movie is "Chief Carlin (Sp?), in the book his name is "Chief Cummings." The movi</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3556678992078564604">Of course, Hollywood doesn't get it right. Each time that I watched the movie, "Fletch"; there is a credit to the book from which the movie comes. Actually, I have had this book in my library for quite some time. Only about a month ago (January 2015), I pulled the book out and read it through. I am not sure why these things happen, but the names of the secondary characters are different. The "Chief of Police" in the movie is "Chief Carlin (Sp?), in the book his name is "Chief Cummings." The movie follows the book mostly, but adds some features that aren't in the book. One such scene is the race down the freeway with the police in 'hot pursuit." In the book, there is no such chase scene. Since I saw the movie first, "Fletch" in the book becomes Chevy Chase. I found it easier to picture the main character with that actor. A good book with good descriptions. Movies are good, but the books from which they come are even better. This book is no exception. "What about you?" "What do you think?"</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_215223645">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/215223645" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5975445-paul" class="left imgcol" title="Paul"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1312651888p2/5975445.jpg" alt="Paul" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/215223645?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 02, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5975445-paul" class="user" title="Paul">Paul</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer215223645">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8550442849783597871">If the rest of the novels in the <em>Fletch</em> series are anything like the first, I'm really surprised they were only able to wring two film versions (and one of them god-awful, at that) out of the franchise. If you've seen the Chevy Chase classic, you know the drill: mystery yarns featuring the quick-witted and faster talking investigative reporter, I.M. Fletcher. <br /><br />In the introductory volume, we meet Fletch as he's in the midst of doing a story on the drug trade at the beach. He's twice divorced and u</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8550442849783597871">If the rest of the novels in the <em>Fletch</em> series are anything like the first, I'm really surprised they were only able to wring two film versions (and one of them god-awful, at that) out of the franchise. If you've seen the Chevy Chase classic, you know the drill: mystery yarns featuring the quick-witted and faster talking investigative reporter, I.M. Fletcher. <br /><br />In the introductory volume, we meet Fletch as he's in the midst of doing a story on the drug trade at the beach. He's twice divorced and under the gun from both his ex-wives' attorneys, as well as the editor who wants him fired. Out of nowhere, he's propositioned to kill Alan Stanwyk, a wealthy corporate vice-president...by Stanwyk himself. Now, Fletch needs to save his job by finding out how a drug dealer who never seems to leave the beach gets his merchandise, keep his wives' attorneys from sending him to jail and find out who Stanwyk really is and why he wants to die...all before he's supposed to kill him at the end of the week.<br /><br />McDonald does a great job maintaining the humor throughout the story despite staying away from slapstick plot points and keeping things (relatively) realistic. Despite being published in 1974, the story and setting doesn't feel dated, at all. Other than the absence of the internet for all Fletch's research, the story could easily be set, yesterday. Definitely looking forward to reading the rest.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8550442849783597871">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_372025143">

  <a href="/user/show/4652026-amber" class="left imgcol" title="Amber"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1292475546p2/4652026.jpg" alt="Amber" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/372025143?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 19, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4652026-amber" class="user" title="Amber">Amber</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4652026-amber?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer372025143'); return false;" href="#">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer372025143">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3168623095726099786">A lot of people whom I respect seem to like this book. I was pretty bored with it though. <br /><br />I think the character of Fletch had potential but there were some aspects of his character (like him throwing his girlfriend's cat out a 7-story window) that I could tell were the author's intention of making the character endearing to the reader but that instead made me dislike him. Maybe I'm just of a different generation but I don't think that was funny. I get it. He's sardonic and cynical with the DGAF</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3168623095726099786">A lot of people whom I respect seem to like this book. I was pretty bored with it though. <br /><br />I think the character of Fletch had potential but there were some aspects of his character (like him throwing his girlfriend's cat out a 7-story window) that I could tell were the author's intention of making the character endearing to the reader but that instead made me dislike him. Maybe I'm just of a different generation but I don't think that was funny. I get it. He's sardonic and cynical with the DGAF attitude but there are other ways to illustrate his dominance and masculinity other than making him cruel to animals. <br /><br />Or maybe it was to illustrate that Fletch hated cats/wasn't a cat person. FINE. I get it. But again, making him throw one out a 7-story window is unnecessary. <br /><br />Now, I tried to ignore and dismiss that gaffe altogether but I just couldn't like the character. He called his boss a 'stupid bitch' to her face, fine. Maybe she was. But I felt like the author didn't write her character thoroughly enough to demonstrate said status of 'stupid bitch' well enough. I felt like the author was trying for shock humor but to me it just came across as weird and uncomfortable. Again, maybe I'm just of a different generation and don't understand the humor.<br /><br />And the shacking up with a 15 year old. Seriously?! That could only be funny to a man who has no knowledge or empathy toward girls who have suffered abuse as children. I wonder if Gregory McDonald has matured emotionally since writing this book.<br /><br />Overall, I know I sound like the PC police but I swear I'm not. I guess this character just sort of touched upon all my specifically wrong nerves!<br /><br />I did, however, like the murder-for-hire mystery/investigation story line (even if it was a little slow-paced). I liked how he talked into the tape recorder and figured things out. His process with solving the case was interesting.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3168623095726099786">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_328156104">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/328156104" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4644695-des" class="left imgcol" title="Des"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" alt="Des" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/328156104?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 11, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4644695-des" class="user" title="Des">Des</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer328156104">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8720041130062162890">Fletch is an investigative reporter with an attitude. Assigned to cover drug dealing on the local beaches, and also hired by a rich man to murder him, Fletch has a lot on his plate. Naturally, he doesn't intend to kill the guy, but he certainly wants to figure out WHY the man wants to be killed. <br /><br />This Fletch book best combines the elements of Fletch's biting, sarcastic humor with a REALISTIC mystery. It's far-fetched, true, but still feels grounded in everyday life. Later Fletch books presented m</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8720041130062162890">Fletch is an investigative reporter with an attitude. Assigned to cover drug dealing on the local beaches, and also hired by a rich man to murder him, Fletch has a lot on his plate. Naturally, he doesn't intend to kill the guy, but he certainly wants to figure out WHY the man wants to be killed. <br /><br />This Fletch book best combines the elements of Fletch's biting, sarcastic humor with a REALISTIC mystery. It's far-fetched, true, but still feels grounded in everyday life. Later Fletch books presented more outrageous mysteries and off-the-wall solutions. They are entertaining, but when you're dealing with mysteries surrounding presidential contenders or people who've undergone sex changes, you see that the "silliness" quotient is upped.<br /><br />But, the main reason to read Fletch is to enjoy the dialogue. Much like the incomparable Ed McBain (Evan Hunter), McDonald can go on for pages with absolutely nothing BUT dialogue. No "said Mr. Jones" or "He laughed." You pick up all the mood, intonation and knowledge of who is speaking simply through the incredibly skillful use of the dialogue. And how often, really, do you laugh outloud when reading novel. You will in this one. I really recommend this book (and all the other Fletch books...Flynn is a little harder to get into.)<br /><br />(A word about the movie...Chevy Chase is NOT the Fletch of these books. The movie's a classic, and even though the movie follows the skeleton of the book's plot, its tone is NOT the same. Enjoy the movie on its own terms but don't let it influence your decision to read the book. If you like the movie, you'll love the book. If you hated the movie, you probably will still like the book.)<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8720041130062162890">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer394906322">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4003341473082249714">Oh, this was so much better than the pre-conception I had gotten from the Chevy Chase flick. So much better.<br /><br />Set, like Chander’s tales, in Southern California, Flech is a hard boiled novel with the ethos of the 70s. Like Chander, Fletch moves through the social strata of Los Angeles easily – junkie, CEO, corrupt cop, the country club set. The author replaced the gum shoe with an investigative reporter (so 70s) but otherwise it’s the same world.<br /><br />It’s an incredibly plotted novel (something Chander c</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4003341473082249714">Oh, this was so much better than the pre-conception I had gotten from the Chevy Chase flick. So much better.<br /><br />Set, like Chander’s tales, in Southern California, Flech is a hard boiled novel with the ethos of the 70s. Like Chander, Fletch moves through the social strata of Los Angeles easily – junkie, CEO, corrupt cop, the country club set. The author replaced the gum shoe with an investigative reporter (so 70s) but otherwise it’s the same world.<br /><br />It’s an incredibly plotted novel (something Chander couldn’t or wouldn’t do) that is still fresh to me. Usually hallmark novels like this get worn out so much by immitation that to go back to the original seems like reading a copy. Nope. Not many have been able to do what was done here.<br /><br />Aside from the plotting the author was able to do the most extraordinary thing. It’s a thing I plan on stealing. The story and novel is told almost entirely in dialogue. The result is a crispy, speedy, compelling read that hauls you along the story without giving you chance to grab a breath. It’s remarkable.<br /></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_32508788">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/32508788" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1415172-joe" class="left imgcol" title="Joe"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1218513852p2/1415172.jpg" alt="Joe" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/32508788?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 10, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1415172-joe" class="user" title="Joe">Joe</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer32508788">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5569974705348562422">One can't help but associate Fletch with Chevy Chase however the novels are a very different style of humor. If you're a fan of mysteries, detective stories and quick-witted, sarcastic dialog, you'll love the Fletch novels. Mcdonald typically uses the same formula for all the Fletch mysteries, which involves two seemingly unrelated events that somehow come together in the end - a formula that consistently works very well in every book. By the 8th or 9th book, you'll be wracking your brain trying</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5569974705348562422">One can't help but associate Fletch with Chevy Chase however the novels are a very different style of humor. If you're a fan of mysteries, detective stories and quick-witted, sarcastic dialog, you'll love the Fletch novels. Mcdonald typically uses the same formula for all the Fletch mysteries, which involves two seemingly unrelated events that somehow come together in the end - a formula that consistently works very well in every book. By the 8th or 9th book, you'll be wracking your brain trying to figure out how random events will eventually tie together to expose the mystery. The sarcastic and quick Irwin Fletcher, with his unorthodox methods and superior investigative journalist skills, will undoubtedly crack the case, make his deadline and still manage to hook up with the hot chick before the bad guys can get away.</span>

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_125842'), "\n\n  &lt;h2&gt;&lt;a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/125842.A_Bolt_from_the_Blue_and_Other_Essays?from_choice=false&amp;amp;from_home_module=false\"&gt;A Bolt from the Blue and Other Essays&lt;\/a&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\n\n      &lt;div&gt;\n        by &lt;a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/7305.Mary_McCarthy?a=7&amp;amp;origin=related_works\"&gt;Mary McCarthy&lt;\/a&gt;\n      &lt;\/div&gt;\n\n          &lt;div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\"&gt;\n            &lt;span class=\"minirating\"&gt;&lt;span class=\"stars staticStars\"&gt;&lt;span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"&gt;&lt;\/span&gt;&lt;span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"&gt;&lt;\/span&gt;&lt;span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"&gt;&lt;\/span&gt;&lt;span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"&gt;&lt;\/span&gt;&lt;span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"&gt;&lt;\/span&gt;&lt;\/span&gt; 3.75 avg rating &amp;mdash; 219 ratings&lt;\/span&gt;            &amp;mdash; published 2001\n          &lt;\/div&gt;\n\n    &lt;div class=\"addBookTipDescription\"&gt;\n      \n&lt;span id=\"freeTextContainer17328624889877579711\"&gt;Mary McCarthy was one of the leading literary figures of her time. In addition to the novels and memoirs for which she is best remembered, she was also a tireless literary and social critic. Starting out as a theater reviewer for \"Partisan Review\" in&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;span id=\"freeText17328624889877579711\" style=\"display:none\"&gt;Mary McCarthy was one of the leading literary figures of her time. In addition to the novels and memoirs for which she is best remembered, she was also a tireless literary and social critic. Starting out as a theater reviewer for &amp;quot;Partisan Review&amp;quot; in 1937, she quickly distinguished herself for her witty and fearless commentary on topics ranging from McCarthyism to the French New Novel to women\'s fashion magazines. McCarthy was an eager controversialist, unsparing in her dissection of anything she found phony or hypocritical. Her reviews are sharp, sometimes malicious, and often very funny, but her criticism is also informed by deep erudition and enlivened by an inexhaustible capacity for enthusiasm. Her political writings, critical in equal measure of the Cold War consensus and of its critics, are less concerned with finding correct positions than with exploring the often absurd circumstances in which agonizing moral decisions are made. While the soundness of McCarthy\'s judgments can sometimes be doubted, her curiosity and intelligence cannot. The intellectual brio and acute judgment that characterizes her best fiction is vividly displayed in this selection of essays, which span McCarthy\'s career from the late 1930s to the late 1970s. It includes her writings on topics such as fashion magazines, Eugene O\'Neill, &amp;quot;A Streetcar Named Desire,&amp;quot; &amp;quot;Look Back in Anger,&amp;quot; &amp;quot;Pale Fire,&amp;quot; J.D. Salinger, Madame Bovary, Italo Calvino, and Watergate. The volume constitutes not only a valuable record of the ideological and cultural controversies that dominated American intellectual life from the Moscow trials to the Watergate hearings, but will also introduce a new generation of readers to a uniquelyforthright and vibrant critical voice.&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;a data-text-id=\"17328624889877579711\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent($(this));; Only recently has there been renewed awareness of the novelist who was such a vital presence in literary Greenwich Village from the 1920s to the&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;span id=\"freeText8447133649028070866\" style=\"display:none\"&gt;For decades after her death, Dawn Powell\'s work was out of print, cherished by a small band of admirers. Only recently has there been renewed awareness of the novelist who was such a vital presence in literary Greenwich Village from the 1920s to the 1960s. With these two volumes, The Library of America presents the best of Powell\'s quirky, often hilarious, sometimes deeply moving fiction.Dawn Powell was the tirelessly observant chronicler of two very different worlds: the small-town Ohio of her childhood and the sophisticated Manhattan to which she gravitated. If her Ohio novels are more melancholy and compassionate in their depiction of often frustrated lives, her Manhattan novels, with their cast of writers, show people, businessmen, and hustling hangers-on, are more exuberant and incisive. But all show rich characterization and a flair for the gist of social complexities. A playful satirist, an unsentimental observer of failed hopes and misguided longings, Dawn Powell is a literary rediscovery of rare importance.&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;a Navigating the perils of both eras is a single cast of characters: Will himself, a young man on the make, eager to do whatever it takes to make his fortune; Will\'s uncle (and sponsor) Gilbert Hawking, a shrewd businessman with one foot in the Old Economy and one in the New; Benjamin Fletcher, the developer of a pioneering new technology destined to set the world on fire; and Theodore Witherspoon, the cheerfully unscrupulous wizard of the financial markets who promises to make them all wealthy beyond their dreams.Meanwhile, Will\'s aspirations are complicated by his pursuit of Ben Fletcher\'s sister, Eliza, the gorgeous and disconcertingly aggressive woman who is as desirable as she is elusive. Can Will succeed in his efforts to win both Eliza and the fortune that her brother\'s new technology seems likely to bring him? She has taken children diving in the Azores to see dolphins and led teenagers on ocean voyages. And she has heard the song of beluga whales in the Arctic Ocean. In Swimming with Giants, Collet describes the power and majesty of being close to some of nature\'s most magnificent creatures. Combining science with a sense of adventure, she conveys the sheer excitement of her work with marine mammals, from the sublime gaze of a whale\'s eye to the race to save animals harmed by pelagic drift nets or toxic spills. A contemporary ecohero in the tradition of Jacques Cousteau, Collet is an inspiration not only for the many who have accompanied her on her research trips, but also for those who will see in her
